      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
        History
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lorentz_force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: History">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tmulti tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:508px;max-width:508px">
        <div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:252px;max-width:252px">
         <div class="thumbimage">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lorentz_force.svg">
           <img alt="" data-file-height="550" data-file-width="675" height="204" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Lorentz_force.svg/250px-Lorentz_force.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Lorentz_force.svg/375px-Lorentz_force.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Lorentz_force.svg/500px-Lorentz_force.svg.png 2x" width="250"/>
          </a>
         </div>
         <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
          Trajectory of a particle with a positive or negative charge
          <i>
           q
          </i>
          under the influence of a magnetic field
          <i>
           B
          </i>
          , which is directed perpendicularly out of the screen.
         </div>
        </div>
        <div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:252px;max-width:252px">
         <div class="thumbimage">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cyclotron_motion.jpg">
           <img alt="" data-file-height="1866" data-file-width="2632" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Cyclotron_motion.jpg/250px-Cyclotron_motion.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Cyclotron_motion.jpg/375px-Cyclotron_motion.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Cyclotron_motion.jpg/500px-Cyclotron_motion.jpg 2x" width="250"/>
          </a>
         </div>
         <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
          Beam of electrons moving in a circle, due to the presence of a magnetic field. Purple light is emitted along the electron path, due to the electrons colliding with gas molecules in the bulb. A
          <a href="/wiki/Teltron_tube" title="Teltron tube">
           Teltron tube
          </a>
          is used in this example.
         </div>
        </div>
        <div style="clear:left">
        </div>
        <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">
         <a href="/wiki/Charged_particle" title="Charged particle">
          Charged particles
         </a>
         experiencing the Lorentz force.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Early attempts to quantitatively describe the electromagnetic force were made in the mid-18th century. It was proposed that the force on magnetic poles, by
       <a href="/wiki/Johann_Tobias_Mayer" title="Johann Tobias Mayer">
        Johann Tobias Mayer
       </a>
       and others in 1760,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and electrically charged objects, by
       <a href="/wiki/Henry_Cavendish" title="Henry Cavendish">
        Henry Cavendish
       </a>
       in 1762,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       obeyed an
       <a href="/wiki/Inverse-square_law" title="Inverse-square law">
        inverse-square law
       </a>
       . However, in both cases the experimental proof was neither complete nor conclusive. It was not until 1784 when
       <a href="/wiki/Charles-Augustin_de_Coulomb" title="Charles-Augustin de Coulomb">
        Charles-Augustin de Coulomb
       </a>
       , using a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Torsion_balance" title="Torsion balance">
        torsion balance
       </a>
       , was able to definitively show through experiment that this was true.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Soon after the discovery in 1820 by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/H._C._%C3%98rsted" title="H. C. Ørsted">
        H. C. Ørsted
       </a>
       that a magnetic needle is acted on by a voltaic current,
       <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9-Marie_Amp%C3%A8re" title="André-Marie Ampère">
        André-Marie Ampère
       </a>
       that same year was able to devise through experimentation the formula for the angular dependence of the force between two current elements.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In all these descriptions, the force was always given in terms of the properties of the objects involved and the distances between them rather than in terms of electric and magnetic fields.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The modern concept of electric and magnetic fields first arose in the theories of
       <a href="/wiki/Michael_Faraday" title="Michael Faraday">
        Michael Faraday
       </a>
       , particularly his idea of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lines_of_force" title="Lines of force">
        lines of force
       </a>
       , later to be given full mathematical description by
       <a href="/wiki/William_Thomson,_1st_Baron_Kelvin" title="William Thomson, 1st Baron Kelvin">
        Lord Kelvin
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/James_Clerk_Maxwell" title="James Clerk Maxwell">
        James Clerk Maxwell
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       From a modern perspective it is possible to identify in Maxwell's 1865 formulation of his field equations a form of the Lorentz force equation in relation to electric currents,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Huray_4-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Huray-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       however, in the time of Maxwell it was not evident how his equations related to the forces on moving charged objects.
       <a href="/wiki/J._J._Thomson" title="J. J. Thomson">
        J. J. Thomson
       </a>
       was the first to attempt to derive from Maxwell's field equations the electromagnetic forces on a moving charged object in terms of the object's properties and external fields. Interested in determining the electromagnetic behavior of the charged particles in
       <a href="/wiki/Cathode_ray" title="Cathode ray">
        cathode rays
       </a>
       , Thomson published a paper in 1881 wherein he gave the force on the particles due to an external magnetic field as
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nahin_3-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Nahin-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbf {F} ={\frac {q}{2}}\mathbf {v} \times \mathbf {B} .}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                F
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mfrac>
                <mi>
                 q
                </mi>
                <mn>
                 2
                </mn>
               </mfrac>
              </mrow>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                v
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               ×
               <!-- × -->
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                B
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               .
              </mo>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle \mathbf {F} ={\frac {q}{2}}\mathbf {v} \times \mathbf {B} .}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="\mathbf {F} ={\frac {q}{2}}\mathbf {v} \times \mathbf {B} ." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ad647751dbbeefaa17da8b1f2f96f881a5255b9e" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:13.579ex; height:4.843ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       Thomson derived the correct basic form of the formula, but, because of some miscalculations and an incomplete description of the
       <a href="/wiki/Displacement_current" title="Displacement current">
        displacement current
       </a>
       , included an incorrect scale-factor of a half in front of the formula.
       <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Heaviside" title="Oliver Heaviside">
        Oliver Heaviside
       </a>
       invented the modern vector notation and applied it to Maxwell's field equations; he also (in 1885 and 1889) had fixed the mistakes of Thomson's derivation and arrived at the correct form of the magnetic force on a moving charged object.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nahin_3-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Nahin-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Finally, in 1895,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dahl_5-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Dahl-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Hendrik_Lorentz" title="Hendrik Lorentz">
        Hendrik Lorentz
       </a>
       derived the modern form of the formula for the electromagnetic force which includes the contributions to the total force from both the electric and the magnetic fields. Lorentz began by abandoning the Maxwellian descriptions of the ether and conduction. Instead, Lorentz made a distinction between matter and the
       <a href="/wiki/Luminiferous_aether" title="Luminiferous aether">
        luminiferous aether
       </a>
       and sought to apply the Maxwell equations at a microscopic scale. Using Heaviside's version of the Maxwell equations for a stationary ether and applying
       <a href="/wiki/Lagrangian_mechanics" title="Lagrangian mechanics">
        Lagrangian mechanics
       </a>
       (see below), Lorentz arrived at the correct and complete form of the force law that now bears his name.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Trajectories_of_particles_due_to_the_Lorentz_force">
        Trajectories of particles due to the Lorentz force
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lorentz_force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Trajectories of particles due to the Lorentz force">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Guiding_center" title="Guiding center">
        Guiding center
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Charged-particle-drifts.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1055" data-file-width="658" height="481" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Charged-particle-drifts.svg/300px-Charged-particle-drifts.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Charged-particle-drifts.svg/450px-Charged-particle-drifts.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Charged-particle-drifts.svg/600px-Charged-particle-drifts.svg.png 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Charged-particle-drifts.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <b>
          Charged particle drifts
         </b>
         in a homogeneous magnetic field. (A) No disturbing force (B) With an electric field, E (C) With an independent force, F (e.g. gravity) (D) In an inhomogeneous magnetic field, grad H
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       In many cases of practical interest, the motion in a
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">
        magnetic field
       </a>
       of an
       <a href="/wiki/Electric_charge" title="Electric charge">
        electrically charged
       </a>
       particle (such as an
       <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">
        electron
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">
        ion
       </a>
       in a
       <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">
        plasma
       </a>
       ) can be treated as the superposition of a relatively fast circular motion around a point called the
       <b>
        guiding center
       </b>
       and a relatively slow
       <b>
        drift
       </b>
       of this point. The drift speeds may differ for various species depending on their charge states, masses, or temperatures, possibly resulting in electric currents or chemical separation.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Significance_of_the_Lorentz_force">
        Significance of the Lorentz force
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lorentz_force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Significance of the Lorentz force">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       While the modern Maxwell's equations describe how electrically charged particles and currents or moving charged particles give rise to electric and magnetic fields, the Lorentz force law completes that picture by describing the force acting on a moving point charge
       <i>
        q
       </i>
       in the presence of electromagnetic fields.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jackson2_6-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Jackson2-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Griffiths50_22-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Griffiths50-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Lorentz force law describes the effect of
       <b>
        E
       </b>
       and
       <b>
        B
       </b>
       upon a point charge, but such electromagnetic forces are not the entire picture. Charged particles are possibly coupled to other forces, notably gravity and nuclear forces. Thus, Maxwell's equations do not stand separate from other physical laws, but are coupled to them via the charge and current densities. The response of a point charge to the Lorentz law is one aspect; the generation of
       <b>
        E
       </b>
       and
       <b>
        B
       </b>
       by currents and charges is another.
      </p>
      <p>
       In real materials the Lorentz force is inadequate to describe the collective behavior of charged particles, both in principle and as a matter of computation. The charged particles in a material medium not only respond to the
       <b>
        E
       </b>
       and
       <b>
        B
       </b>
       fields but also generate these fields. Complex transport equations must be solved to determine the time and spatial response of charges, for example, the
       <a href="/wiki/Boltzmann_equation" title="Boltzmann equation">
        Boltzmann equation
       </a>
       or the
       <a href="/wiki/Fokker%E2%80%93Planck_equation" title="Fokker–Planck equation">
        Fokker–Planck equation
       </a>
       or the
       <a href="/wiki/Navier%E2%80%93Stokes_equations" title="Navier–Stokes equations">
        Navier–Stokes equations
       </a>
       . For example, see
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetohydrodynamics" title="Magnetohydrodynamics">
        magnetohydrodynamics
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Fluid_dynamics" title="Fluid dynamics">
        fluid dynamics
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Electrohydrodynamics" title="Electrohydrodynamics">
        electrohydrodynamics
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Superconductivity" title="Superconductivity">
        superconductivity
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Stellar_evolution" title="Stellar evolution">
        stellar evolution
       </a>
       . An entire physical apparatus for dealing with these matters has developed. See for example,
       <a href="/wiki/Green%E2%80%93Kubo_relations" title="Green–Kubo relations">
        Green–Kubo relations
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Green%27s_function_(many-body_theory)" title="Green's function (many-body theory)">
        Green's function (many-body theory)
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Lorentz_force_law_as_the_definition_of_E_and_B">
        Lorentz force law as the definition of E and B
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lorentz_force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Lorentz force law as the definition of E and B">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       In many textbook treatments of classical electromagnetism, the Lorentz force Law is used as the
       <i>
        definition
       </i>
       of the electric and magnetic fields
       <b>
        E
       </b>
       and
       <b>
        B
       </b>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jackson20_23-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Jackson20-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
        <a href="#cite_note-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
        <a href="#cite_note-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       To be specific, the Lorentz force is understood to be the following empirical statement:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         The electromagnetic force
         <b>
          F
         </b>
         on a
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Test_charge" title="Test charge">
          test charge
         </a>
         at a given point and time is a certain function of its charge
        </i>
        q
        <i>
         and velocity
         <b>
          v
         </b>
         , which can be parameterized by exactly two vectors
         <b>
          E
         </b>
         and
         <b>
          B
         </b>
         , in the functional form
        </i>
        :
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <dl>
         <dd>
          <span class="mwe-math-element">
           <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
            <math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbf {F} =q(\mathbf {E} +\mathbf {v} \times \mathbf {B} )}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
             <semantics>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                 <mi mathvariant="bold">
                  F
                 </mi>
                </mrow>
                <mo>
                 =
                </mo>
                <mi>
                 q
                </mi>
                <mo stretchy="false">
                 (
                </mo>
                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                 <mi mathvariant="bold">
                  E
                 </mi>
                </mrow>
                <mo>
                 +
                </mo>
                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                 <mi mathvariant="bold">
                  v
                 </mi>
                </mrow>
                <mo>
                 ×
                 <!-- × -->
                </mo>
                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                 <mi mathvariant="bold">
                  B
                 </mi>
                </mrow>
                <mo stretchy="false">
                 )
                </mo>
               </mstyle>
              </mrow>
              <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
               {\displaystyle \mathbf {F} =q(\mathbf {E} +\mathbf {v} \times \mathbf {B} )}
              </annotation>
             </semantics>
            </math>
           </span>
           <img alt="\mathbf {F} =q(\mathbf {E} +\mathbf {v} \times \mathbf {B} )" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a3dd99e3bd55cbeff1cd2506d944405f3efa9e0c" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:18.41ex; height:2.843ex;"/>
          </span>
         </dd>
        </dl>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       This is valid, even for particles approaching the speed of light (that is,
       <a href="/wiki/Norm_(mathematics)#Euclidean_norm" title="Norm (mathematics)">
        magnitude
       </a>
       of
       <b>
        v
       </b>
       = |
       <b>
        v
       </b>
       | ≈
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
        <a href="#cite_note-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       So the two
       <a href="/wiki/Vector_field" title="Vector field">
        vector fields
       </a>
       <b>
        E
       </b>
       and
       <b>
        B
       </b>
       are thereby defined throughout space and time, and these are called the "electric field" and "magnetic field". The fields are defined everywhere in space and time with respect to what force a test charge would receive regardless of whether a charge is present to experience the force.
      </p>
      <p>
       As a definition of
       <b>
        E
       </b>
       and
       <b>
        B
       </b>
       , the Lorentz force is only a definition in principle because a real particle (as opposed to the hypothetical "test charge" of infinitesimally-small mass and charge) would generate its own finite
       <b>
        E
       </b>
       and
       <b>
        B
       </b>
       fields, which would alter the electromagnetic force that it experiences. In addition, if the charge experiences acceleration, as if forced into a curved trajectory by some external agency, it emits radiation that causes braking of its motion. See for example
       <a href="/wiki/Bremsstrahlung" title="Bremsstrahlung">
        Bremsstrahlung
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synchrotron_light" title="Synchrotron light">
        synchrotron light
       </a>
       . These effects occur through both a direct effect (called the
       <a href="/wiki/Abraham%E2%80%93Lorentz_force" title="Abraham–Lorentz force">
        radiation reaction force
       </a>
       ) and indirectly (by affecting the motion of nearby charges and currents). Moreover, net force must include
       <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">
        gravity
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Electroweak_interaction" title="Electroweak interaction">
        electroweak
       </a>
       , and any other forces aside from electromagnetic force.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Force_on_a_current-carrying_wire">
        Force on a current-carrying wire
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lorentz_force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Force on a current-carrying wire">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Regla_mano_derecha_Laplace.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="202" data-file-width="363" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Regla_mano_derecha_Laplace.svg/250px-Regla_mano_derecha_Laplace.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Regla_mano_derecha_Laplace.svg/375px-Regla_mano_derecha_Laplace.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Regla_mano_derecha_Laplace.svg/500px-Regla_mano_derecha_Laplace.svg.png 2x" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Regla_mano_derecha_Laplace.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Right-hand rule for a current-carrying wire in a magnetic field
         <i>
          B
         </i>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       When a wire carrying an electric current is placed in a magnetic field, each of the moving charges, which comprise the current, experiences the Lorentz force, and together they can create a macroscopic force on the wire (sometimes called the
       <b>
        Laplace force
       </b>
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2014)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       ). By combining the Lorentz force law above with the definition of electric current, the following equation results, in the case of a straight, stationary wire:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbf {F} =I{\boldsymbol {\ell }}\times \mathbf {B} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                F
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mi>
               I
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold-italic">
                ℓ
                <!-- ℓ -->
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               ×
               <!-- × -->
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                B
               </mi>
              </mrow>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle \mathbf {F} =I{\boldsymbol {\ell }}\times \mathbf {B} }
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="{\displaystyle \mathbf {F} =I{\boldsymbol {\ell }}\times \mathbf {B} }" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ff81a0ae2712583aa64bd09bfe42a875cd108232" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:11.796ex; height:2.176ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       where
       <b>
        ℓ
       </b>
       is a vector whose magnitude is the length of wire, and whose direction is along the wire, aligned with the direction of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conventional_current" title="Conventional current">
        conventional current
       </a>
       flow
       <i>
        I
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       If the wire is not straight but curved, the force on it can be computed by applying this formula to each
       <a href="/wiki/Infinitesimal" title="Infinitesimal">
        infinitesimal
       </a>
       segment of wire
       <i>
        d
       </i>
       <b>
        ℓ
       </b>
       , then adding up all these forces by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Integration_(calculus)" title="Integration (calculus)">
        integration
       </a>
       . Formally, the net force on a stationary, rigid wire carrying a steady current
       <i>
        I
       </i>
       is
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbf {F} =I\int \mathrm {d} {\boldsymbol {\ell }}\times \mathbf {B} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                F
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mi>
               I
              </mi>
              <mo>
               ∫
               <!-- ∫ -->
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="normal">
                d
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold-italic">
                ℓ
                <!-- ℓ -->
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               ×
               <!-- × -->
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                B
               </mi>
              </mrow>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle \mathbf {F} =I\int \mathrm {d} {\boldsymbol {\ell }}\times \mathbf {B} }
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="\mathbf {F} =I\int \mathrm {d} {\boldsymbol {\ell }}\times \mathbf {B} " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/64251587648884ef7bf4243d6710a14172cedc8b" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:16.057ex; height:5.676ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       This is the net force. In addition, there will usually be
       <a href="/wiki/Torque" title="Torque">
        torque
       </a>
       , plus other effects if the wire is not perfectly rigid.
      </p>
      <p>
       One application of this is
       <a href="/wiki/Amp%C3%A8re%27s_force_law" title="Ampère's force law">
        Ampère's force law
       </a>
       , which describes how two current-carrying wires can attract or repel each other, since each experiences a Lorentz force from the other's magnetic field. For more information, see the article:
       <a href="/wiki/Amp%C3%A8re%27s_force_law" title="Ampère's force law">
        Ampère's force law
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="EMF">
        EMF
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lorentz_force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: EMF">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The magnetic force (
       <span class="nowrap">
        <i>
         q
        </i>
        <b>
         v
        </b>
        ×
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       ) component of the Lorentz force is responsible for
       <i>
        motional
       </i>
       <a href="/wiki/Electromotive_force" title="Electromotive force">
        electromotive force
       </a>
       (or
       <i>
        motional EMF
       </i>
       ), the phenomenon underlying many electrical generators. When a conductor is moved through a magnetic field, the magnetic field exerts opposite forces on electrons and nuclei in the wire, and this creates the EMF. The term "motional EMF" is applied to this phenomenon, since the EMF is due to the
       <i>
        motion
       </i>
       of the wire.
      </p>
      <p>
       In other electrical generators, the magnets move, while the conductors do not. In this case, the EMF is due to the electric force (
       <i>
        q
       </i>
       <b>
        E
       </b>
       ) term in the Lorentz Force equation. The electric field in question is created by the changing magnetic field, resulting in an
       <i>
        induced
       </i>
       EMF, as described by the
       <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_induction#Maxwell–Faraday_equation" title="Electromagnetic induction">
        Maxwell–Faraday equation
       </a>
       (one of the four modern
       <a href="/wiki/Maxwell%27s_equations" title="Maxwell's equations">
        Maxwell's equations
       </a>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Griffiths302_27-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Griffiths302-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Both of these EMFs, despite their apparently distinct origins, are described by the same equation, namely, the EMF is the rate of change of
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_flux" title="Magnetic flux">
        magnetic flux
       </a>
       through the wire. (This is Faraday's law of induction, see
       <a href="/wiki/Lorentz_force#Lorentz_force_and_Faraday.27s_law_of_induction" title="Lorentz force">
        below
       </a>
       .) Einstein's
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Special_theory_of_relativity" title="Special theory of relativity">
        special theory of relativity
       </a>
       was partially motivated by the desire to better understand this link between the two effects.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Griffiths302_27-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Griffiths302-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In fact, the electric and magnetic fields are different facets of the same electromagnetic field, and in moving from one inertial frame to another, the
       <a href="/wiki/Solenoidal_vector_field" title="Solenoidal vector field">
        solenoidal vector field
       </a>
       portion of the
       <i>
        E
       </i>
       -field can change in whole or in part to a
       <i>
        B
       </i>
       -field or
       <i>
        vice versa
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chow_28-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Chow-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span id="Lorentz_force_and_Faraday.27s_law_of_induction">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Lorentz_force_and_Faraday's_law_of_induction">
        Lorentz force and Faraday's law of induction
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lorentz_force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Lorentz force and Faraday's law of induction">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:263px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LeidenLorentz1.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3648" data-file-width="5472" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/LeidenLorentz1.jpg/261px-LeidenLorentz1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/LeidenLorentz1.jpg/392px-LeidenLorentz1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/LeidenLorentz1.jpg/522px-LeidenLorentz1.jpg 2x" width="261"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LeidenLorentz1.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Lorentz force -image on a wall in Leiden
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Faraday%27s_law_of_induction" title="Faraday's law of induction">
        Faraday's law of induction
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Given a loop of wire in a
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">
        magnetic field
       </a>
       , Faraday's law of induction states the induced
       <a href="/wiki/Electromotive_force" title="Electromotive force">
        electromotive force
       </a>
       (EMF) in the wire is:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle {\mathcal {E}}=-{\frac {\mathrm {d} \Phi _{B}}{\mathrm {d} t}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi class="MJX-tex-caligraphic" mathvariant="script">
                 E
                </mi>
               </mrow>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mo>
               −
               <!-- − -->
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mfrac>
                <mrow>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mi mathvariant="normal">
                   d
                  </mi>
                 </mrow>
                 <msub>
                  <mi mathvariant="normal">
                   Φ
                   <!-- Φ -->
                  </mi>
                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                   <mi>
                    B
                   </mi>
                  </mrow>
                 </msub>
                </mrow>
                <mrow>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mi mathvariant="normal">
                   d
                  </mi>
                 </mrow>
                 <mi>
                  t
                 </mi>
                </mrow>
               </mfrac>
              </mrow>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle {\mathcal {E}}=-{\frac {\mathrm {d} \Phi _{B}}{\mathrm {d} t}}}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="{\mathcal {E}}=-{\frac {\mathrm {d} \Phi _{B}}{\mathrm {d} t}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9e6407cbe63c6c6e822b7874c94518a66eb08bb2" style="vertical-align: -2.005ex; width:11.504ex; height:5.509ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       where
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle \Phi _{B}=\iint _{\Sigma (t)}\mathrm {d} \mathbf {A} \cdot \mathbf {B} (\mathbf {r} ,t)}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <msub>
               <mi mathvariant="normal">
                Φ
                <!-- Φ -->
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi>
                 B
                </mi>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <msub>
               <mo>
                ∬
                <!-- ∬ -->
               </mo>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi mathvariant="normal">
                 Σ
                 <!-- Σ -->
                </mi>
                <mo stretchy="false">
                 (
                </mo>
                <mi>
                 t
                </mi>
                <mo stretchy="false">
                 )
                </mo>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="normal">
                d
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                A
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               ⋅
               <!-- ⋅ -->
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                B
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo stretchy="false">
               (
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                r
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               ,
              </mo>
              <mi>
               t
              </mi>
              <mo stretchy="false">
               )
              </mo>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle \Phi _{B}=\iint _{\Sigma (t)}\mathrm {d} \mathbf {A} \cdot \mathbf {B} (\mathbf {r} ,t)}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="\Phi _{B}=\iint _{\Sigma (t)}\mathrm {d} \mathbf {A} \cdot \mathbf {B} (\mathbf {r} ,t)" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/65953cbb66aa08cfced5acd21f7a1ee9935c67b3" style="vertical-align: -2.671ex; width:24.131ex; height:6.009ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       is the
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_flux" title="Magnetic flux">
        magnetic flux
       </a>
       through the loop,
       <b>
        B
       </b>
       is the magnetic field, Σ(
       <i>
        t
       </i>
       ) is a surface bounded by the closed contour ∂Σ(
       <i>
        t
       </i>
       ), at all at time
       <i>
        t
       </i>
       , d
       <b>
        A
       </b>
       is an infinitesimal
       <a href="/wiki/Vector_area" title="Vector area">
        vector area
       </a>
       element of Σ(
       <i>
        t
       </i>
       ) (magnitude is the area of an infinitesimal patch of surface, direction is
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orthogonal" title="Orthogonal">
        orthogonal
       </a>
       to that surface patch).
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <i>
        sign
       </i>
       of the EMF is determined by
       <a href="/wiki/Lenz%27s_law" title="Lenz's law">
        Lenz's law
       </a>
       . Note that this is valid for not only a
       <i>
        stationary
       </i>
       wire – but also for a
       <i>
        moving
       </i>
       wire.
      </p>
      <p>
       From
       <a href="/wiki/Faraday%27s_law_of_induction" title="Faraday's law of induction">
        Faraday's law of induction
       </a>
       (that is valid for a moving wire, for instance in a motor) and the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maxwell_Equations" title="Maxwell Equations">
        Maxwell Equations
       </a>
       , the Lorentz Force can be deduced. The reverse is also true, the Lorentz force and the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maxwell_Equations" title="Maxwell Equations">
        Maxwell Equations
       </a>
       can be used to derive the
       <a href="/wiki/Faraday%27s_law_of_induction" title="Faraday's law of induction">
        Faraday Law
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Let Σ(
       <i>
        t
       </i>
       ) be the moving wire, moving together without rotation and with constant velocity
       <b>
        v
       </b>
       and Σ(
       <i>
        t
       </i>
       ) be the internal surface of the wire. The EMF around the closed path ∂Σ(
       <i>
        t
       </i>
       ) is given by:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Landau_29-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Landau-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle {\mathcal {E}}=\oint _{\partial \Sigma (t)}\mathrm {d} {\boldsymbol {\ell }}\cdot \mathbf {F} /q}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi class="MJX-tex-caligraphic" mathvariant="script">
                 E
                </mi>
               </mrow>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <msub>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-OP MJX-fixedlimits">
                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-VCENTER">
                 <mstyle mathsize="2.07em">
                  <mtext>
                   ∮
                   <!-- ∮ -->
                  </mtext>
                  <mspace width="thinmathspace">
                  </mspace>
                 </mstyle>
                </mrow>
                <mspace width="thinmathspace">
                </mspace>
               </mrow>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi mathvariant="normal">
                 ∂
                 <!-- ∂ -->
                </mi>
                <mi mathvariant="normal">
                 Σ
                 <!-- Σ -->
                </mi>
                <mo stretchy="false">
                 (
                </mo>
                <mi>
                 t
                </mi>
                <mo stretchy="false">
                 )
                </mo>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
              <mo>
               ⁡
               <!-- ⁡ -->
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="normal">
                d
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold-italic">
                ℓ
                <!-- ℓ -->
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               ⋅
               <!-- ⋅ -->
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                F
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mo>
                /
               </mo>
              </mrow>
              <mi>
               q
              </mi>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle {\mathcal {E}}=\oint _{\partial \Sigma (t)}\mathrm {d} {\boldsymbol {\ell }}\cdot \mathbf {F} /q}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="{\mathcal {E}}=\oint _{\partial \Sigma (t)}\mathrm {d} {\boldsymbol {\ell }}\cdot \mathbf {F} /q" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8cc7eb34babc8aa2e16411ac0b38b83bd086a824" style="vertical-align: -2.838ex; width:20.718ex; height:6.343ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       where
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbf {E} =\mathbf {F} /q}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                E
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                F
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mo>
                /
               </mo>
              </mrow>
              <mi>
               q
              </mi>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle \mathbf {E} =\mathbf {F} /q}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="\mathbf {E} =\mathbf {F} /q" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f4dc12744635bc154b7c700d69df110ea618dafa" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:8.77ex; height:2.843ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       is the electric field and d
       <b>
        ℓ
       </b>
       is an
       <a href="/wiki/Infinitesimal" title="Infinitesimal">
        infinitesimal
       </a>
       vector element of the contour ∂Σ(
       <i>
        t
       </i>
       ).
      </p>
      <p>
       NB: Both d
       <b>
        ℓ
       </b>
       and d
       <b>
        A
       </b>
       have a sign ambiguity; to get the correct sign, the
       <a href="/wiki/Right-hand_rule" title="Right-hand rule">
        right-hand rule
       </a>
       is used, as explained in the article
       <a href="/wiki/Kelvin%E2%80%93Stokes_theorem" title="Kelvin–Stokes theorem">
        Kelvin–Stokes theorem
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The above result can be compared with the version of Faraday's law of induction that appears in the modern Maxwell's equations, called here the
       <i>
        Maxwell–Faraday equation
       </i>
       :
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">

              </mo>

               <!-- − -->
              </mo>

      <p>
       The Maxwell–Faraday equation also can be written in an
       <i>
        integral form
       </i>
       using the
       <a href="/wiki/Kelvin%E2%80%93Stokes_theorem" title="Kelvin–Stokes theorem">
        Kelvin–Stokes theorem
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harrington_30-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Harrington-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>

            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle \oint _{\partial \Sigma (t)}\mathrm {d} {\boldsymbol {\ell }}\cdot \mathbf {F} /q(\mathbf {r} ,\ t)=\oint _{\partial \Sigma (t)}\mathrm {d} {\boldsymbol {\ell }}\cdot \mathbf {E} (\mathbf {r} ,\ t)+\oint _{\partial \Sigma (t)}\!\!\!\!\mathbf {v} \times \mathbf {B} (\mathbf {r} ,\ t)\,\mathrm {d} {\boldsymbol {\ell }}}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="\oint _{\partial \Sigma (t)}\mathrm {d} {\boldsymbol {\ell }}\cdot \mathbf {F} /q(\mathbf {r} ,\ t)=\oint _{\partial \Sigma (t)}\mathrm {d} {\boldsymbol {\ell }}\cdot \mathbf {E} (\mathbf {r} ,\ t)+\oint _{\partial \Sigma (t)}\!\!\!\!\mathbf {v} \times \mathbf {B} (\mathbf {r} ,\ t)\,\mathrm {d} {\boldsymbol {\ell }}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7f51c94587154c74071f2fa6031c434f28fd20c8" style="vertical-align: -2.838ex; width:67.814ex; height:6.343ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       since this is valid for any wire position it implies that,
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbf {F} =q\,\mathbf {E} (\mathbf {r} ,\ t)+q\,\mathbf {v} \times \mathbf {B} (\mathbf {r} ,\ t).}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                F
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mi>
               q
              </mi>
              <mspace width="thinmathspace">
              </mspace>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                E
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo stretchy="false">
               (
              </mo>

              </mi>

               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                v
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               ×
               <!-- × -->
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                B
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo stretchy="false">
               (
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                r
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               ,
              </mo>
              <mtext>
              </mtext>
              <mi>
               t
              </mi>
              <mo stretchy="false">

              </mo>
              <mo>

              </mo>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle \mathbf {F} =q\,\mathbf {E} (\mathbf {r} ,\ t)+q\,\mathbf {v} \times \mathbf {B} (\mathbf {r} ,\ t).}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="\mathbf {F} =q\,\mathbf {E} (\mathbf {r} ,\ t)+q\,\mathbf {v} \times \mathbf {B} (\mathbf {r} ,\ t)." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d74233ac067e8a2b94fbe4ff58f0e7df2f8f15e1" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:29.822ex; height:2.843ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       Faraday's law of induction holds whether the loop of wire is rigid and stationary, or in motion or in process of deformation, and it holds whether the magnetic field is constant in time or changing. However, there are cases where Faraday's law is either inadequate or difficult to use, and application of the underlying Lorentz force law is necessary. See
       <a href="/wiki/Faraday_paradox#Inapplicability_of_Faraday's_law" title="Faraday paradox">
        inapplicability of Faraday's law
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       If the magnetic field is fixed in time and the conducting loop moves through the field, the magnetic flux Φ
       <sub>
        <i>
         B
        </i>
       </sub>
       linking the loop can change in several ways. For example, if the
       <b>
        B
       </b>
       -field varies with position, and the loop moves to a location with different
       <b>
        B
       </b>
       -field, Φ
       <sub>
        <i>
         B
        </i>
       </sub>
       will change. Alternatively, if the loop changes orientation with respect to the
       <b>
        B
       </b>
       -field, the
       <span class="nowrap">
        <b>
         B
        </b>
        ⋅ d
        <b>
         A
        </b>
       </span>
       differential element will change because of the different angle between
       <b>
        B
       </b>
       and d
       <b>
        A
       </b>
       , also changing Φ
       <sub>
        <i>
         B
        </i>
       </sub>
       . As a third example, if a portion of the circuit is swept through a uniform, time-independent
       <b>
        B
       </b>
       -field, and another portion of the circuit is held stationary, the flux linking the entire closed circuit can change due to the shift in relative position of the circuit's component parts with time (surface ∂Σ(
       <i>
        t
       </i>
       ) time-dependent). In all three cases, Faraday's law of induction then predicts the EMF generated by the change in Φ
       <sub>
        <i>
         B
        </i>
       </sub>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Note that the Maxwell Faraday's equation implies that the Electric Field
       <b>
        E
       </b>
       is non conservative when the Magnetic Field
       <b>
        B
       </b>
       varies in time, and is not expressible as the gradient of a
       <a href="/wiki/Scalar_field" title="Scalar field">
        scalar field
       </a>
       , and not subject to the
       <a href="/wiki/Gradient_theorem" title="Gradient theorem">
        gradient theorem
       </a>
       since its rotational is not zero.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Landau_29-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Landau-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
        <a href="#cite_note-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Lorentz_force_in_terms_of_potentials">
        Lorentz force in terms of potentials
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lorentz_force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Lorentz force in terms of potentials">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_descriptions_of_the_electromagnetic_field" title="Mathematical descriptions of the electromagnetic field">
        Mathematical descriptions of the electromagnetic field
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Maxwell%27s_equations" title="Maxwell's equations">
        Maxwell's equations
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Helmholtz_decomposition" title="Helmholtz decomposition">
        Helmholtz decomposition
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The
       <b>
        E
       </b>
       and
       <b>
        B
       </b>
       fields can be replaced by the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetic_vector_potential" title="Magnetic vector potential">

       </a>
       <b>

       </b>

       </i>

      </p>

             <img alt="\mathbf {F} =q\left[-\nabla \phi -{\frac {\partial \mathbf {A} }{\partial t}}+\nabla (\mathbf {v} \cdot \mathbf {A} )-(\mathbf {v} \cdot \nabla )\mathbf {A} \right]" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a7fd3adddbdfb95797d11ef6167ecda4efe3e0b9" style="vertical-align: -2.505ex; width:44.227ex; height:6.176ex;"/>
            </span>
           </p>
          </td>
         </tr>
        </table>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       using the chain rule, the
       <a href="/wiki/Total_derivative" title="Total derivative">
        total derivative
       </a>
       of
       <b>
        A
       </b>
       is:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle {\frac {\mathrm {d} \mathbf {A} }{\mathrm {d} t}}={\frac {\partial \mathbf {A} }{\partial t}}+(\mathbf {v} \cdot \nabla )\mathbf {A} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mfrac>
                <mrow>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mi mathvariant="normal">
                   d
                  </mi>
                 </mrow>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mi mathvariant="bold">
                   A
                  </mi>
                 </mrow>
                </mrow>
                <mrow>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mi mathvariant="normal">
                   d
                  </mi>
                 </mrow>
                 <mi>
                  t
                 </mi>
                </mrow>
               </mfrac>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mfrac>
                <mrow>
                 <mi mathvariant="normal">
                  ∂
                  <!-- ∂ -->
                 </mi>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mi mathvariant="bold">
                   A
                  </mi>
                 </mrow>
                </mrow>
                <mrow>
                 <mi mathvariant="normal">
                  ∂
                  <!-- ∂ -->
                 </mi>
                 <mi>
                  t
                 </mi>
                </mrow>
               </mfrac>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               +
              </mo>
              <mo stretchy="false">
               (
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                v
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               ⋅
               <!-- ⋅ -->
              </mo>
              <mi mathvariant="normal">
               ∇
               <!-- ∇ -->
              </mi>
              <mo stretchy="false">
               )
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                A
               </mi>
              </mrow>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle {\frac {\mathrm {d} \mathbf {A} }{\mathrm {d} t}}={\frac {\partial \mathbf {A} }{\partial t}}+(\mathbf {v} \cdot \nabla )\mathbf {A} }
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="{\frac {\mathrm {d} \mathbf {A} }{\mathrm {d} t}}={\frac {\partial \mathbf {A} }{\partial t}}+(\mathbf {v} \cdot \nabla )\mathbf {A} " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/01451a9d2e2842cdc0a8ecc2e9efbc8f0db98ebf" style="vertical-align: -2.005ex; width:23.115ex; height:5.509ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       so the above expression can be rewritten as;
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">

          <math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbf {F} =q\left[-\nabla (\phi -\mathbf {v} \cdot \mathbf {A} )-{\frac {d\mathbf {A} }{\mathrm {d} t}}\right]}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">

               </mi>

              </mo>

              </mi>

               </mo>

                 <!-- − -->
                </mo>

                 <!-- ∇ -->
                </mi>

                </mo>

                 <!-- ϕ -->
                </mi>
                <mo>

          </math>

         <img alt="\mathbf {F} =q\left[-\nabla (\phi -\mathbf {v} \cdot \mathbf {A} )-{\frac {d\mathbf {A} }{\mathrm {d} t}}\right]" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/80711365244060be993f180b7af7b437d56fc868" style="vertical-align: -2.505ex; width:30.493ex; height:6.176ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>

       which can take the convenient Euler–Lagrange form

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Lorentz_force_and_analytical_mechanics">
        Lorentz force and analytical mechanics
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lorentz_force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Lorentz force and analytical mechanics">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Momentum#Lagrangian_and_Hamiltonian_formulation" title="Momentum">
        Momentum
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Lagrangian_mechanics" title="Lagrangian mechanics">
        Lagrangian
       </a>
       for a charged particle of mass
       <i>
        m
       </i>
       and charge
       <i>
        q
       </i>
       in an electromagnetic field equivalently describes the dynamics of the particle in terms of its
       <i>
        energy
       </i>
       , rather than the force exerted on it. The classical expression is given by:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle L={\frac {m}{2}}\mathbf {\dot {r}} \cdot \mathbf {\dot {r}} +q\mathbf {A} \cdot \mathbf {\dot {r}} -q\phi }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mi>
               L
              </mi>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mfrac>
                <mi>
                 m
                </mi>
                <mn>
                 2
                </mn>
               </mfrac>
              </mrow>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mover>
                 <mi mathvariant="bold">
                  r
                 </mi>
                 <mo mathvariant="bold">
                  ˙
                  <!-- ˙ -->
                 </mo>
                </mover>
               </mrow>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               ⋅
               <!-- ⋅ -->
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mover>
                 <mi mathvariant="bold">
                  r
                 </mi>
                 <mo mathvariant="bold">
                  ˙
                  <!-- ˙ -->
                 </mo>
                </mover>
               </mrow>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               +
              </mo>
              <mi>
               q
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                A
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               ⋅
               <!-- ⋅ -->
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mover>
                 <mi mathvariant="bold">
                  r
                 </mi>
                 <mo mathvariant="bold">
                  ˙
                  <!-- ˙ -->
                 </mo>
                </mover>
               </mrow>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               −
               <!-- − -->
              </mo>
              <mi>
               q
              </mi>
              <mi>
               ϕ
               <!-- ϕ -->
              </mi>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle L={\frac {m}{2}}\mathbf {\dot {r}} \cdot \mathbf {\dot {r}} +q\mathbf {A} \cdot \mathbf {\dot {r}} -q\phi }
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="L={\frac {m}{2}}\mathbf {\dot {r}} \cdot \mathbf {\dot {r}} +q\mathbf {A} \cdot \mathbf {\dot {r}} -q\phi " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4f273b89bde7d31aaa4b0e2a9733029add74ee50" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:26.151ex; height:4.676ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       where
       <b>
        A
       </b>
       and
       <i>
        ϕ
       </i>
       are the potential fields as above. Using
       <a href="/wiki/Lagrangian_mechanics" title="Lagrangian mechanics">
        Lagrange's equations
       </a>
       , the equation for the Lorentz force can be obtained.
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <table class="toccolours collapsible collapsed" style="text-align:left" width="80%">
         <tr>
          <th>
           Derivation of Lorentz force from classical Lagrangian (SI units)
          </th>
         </tr>
         <tr>
          <td>
           For an
           <b>
            A
           </b>
           field, a particle moving with velocity
           <b>
            v
           </b>
           =
           <b>
            ṙ
           </b>
           has
           <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Potential_momentum" title="Potential momentum">
            potential momentum
           </a>
           <span class="mwe-math-element">
            <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
             <math alttext="{\displaystyle q\mathbf {A} (\mathbf {r} ,t)}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
              <semantics>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
                 <mi>
                  q
                 </mi>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mi mathvariant="bold">
                   A
                  </mi>
                 </mrow>
                 <mo stretchy="false">
                  (
                 </mo>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mi mathvariant="bold">
                   r
                  </mi>
                 </mrow>
                 <mo>
                  ,
                 </mo>
                 <mi>
                  t
                 </mi>
                 <mo stretchy="false">
                  )
                 </mo>
                </mstyle>
               </mrow>
               <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
                {\displaystyle q\mathbf {A} (\mathbf {r} ,t)}
               </annotation>
              </semantics>
             </math>
            </span>
            <img alt="q\mathbf {A} (\mathbf {r} ,t)" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b0ec518dca68a0cbf6404d86b996a9dee5ccf0e1" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:7.874ex; height:2.843ex;"/>
           </span>
           , so its potential energy is
           <span class="mwe-math-element">
            <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
             <math alttext="{\displaystyle q\mathbf {A} (\mathbf {r} ,t)\cdot \mathbf {\dot {r}} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
              <semantics>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
                 <mi>
                  q
                 </mi>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mi mathvariant="bold">
                   A
                  </mi>
                 </mrow>
                 <mo stretchy="false">
                  (
                 </mo>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mi mathvariant="bold">
                   r
                  </mi>
                 </mrow>
                 <mo>
                  ,
                 </mo>
                 <mi>
                  t
                 </mi>
                 <mo stretchy="false">
                  )
                 </mo>
                 <mo>
                  ⋅
                  <!-- ⋅ -->
                 </mo>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                   <mover>
                    <mi mathvariant="bold">
                     r
                    </mi>
                    <mo mathvariant="bold">
                     ˙
                     <!-- ˙ -->
                    </mo>
                   </mover>
                  </mrow>
                 </mrow>
                </mstyle>
               </mrow>
               <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
                {\displaystyle q\mathbf {A} (\mathbf {r} ,t)\cdot \mathbf {\dot {r}} }
               </annotation>
              </semantics>
             </math>
            </span>
            <img alt="q\mathbf {A} (\mathbf {r} ,t)\cdot \mathbf {\dot {r}} " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/459f8482acf59beaeabc56409537140a2d1e987a" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:10.89ex; height:2.843ex;"/>
           </span>
           . For a
           <i>
            ϕ
           </i>
           field, the particle's potential energy is
           <span class="mwe-math-element">
            <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
             <math alttext="{\displaystyle q\phi (\mathbf {r} ,t)}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
              <semantics>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
                 <mi>
                  q
                 </mi>
                 <mi>
                  ϕ
                  <!-- ϕ -->
                 </mi>
                 <mo stretchy="false">
                  (
                 </mo>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mi mathvariant="bold">
                   r
                  </mi>
                 </mrow>
                 <mo>
                  ,
                 </mo>
                 <mi>
                  t
                 </mi>
                 <mo stretchy="false">
                  )
                 </mo>
                </mstyle>
               </mrow>
               <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
                {\displaystyle q\phi (\mathbf {r} ,t)}
               </annotation>
              </semantics>
             </math>
            </span>
            <img alt="q\phi (\mathbf {r} ,t)" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/cd84d01ab8dfe0a6e33bc554ca8f235af44f8ac2" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:7.24ex; height:2.843ex;"/>
           </span>
           .
           <p>
            The total
            <a href="/wiki/Potential_energy" title="Potential energy">
             potential energy
            </a>
            is then:
           </p>
           <dl>
            <dd>
             <span class="mwe-math-element">
              <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
               <math alttext="{\displaystyle V=q\phi -q\mathbf {A} \cdot \mathbf {\dot {r}} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
                <semantics>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
                   <mi>
                    V
                   </mi>
                   <mo>
                    =
                   </mo>
                   <mi>
                    q
                   </mi>
                   <mi>
                    ϕ
                    <!-- ϕ -->
                   </mi>
                   <mo>
                    −
                    <!-- − -->
                   </mo>
                   <mi>
                    q
                   </mi>
                   <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                    <mi mathvariant="bold">
                     A
                    </mi>
                   </mrow>
                   <mo>
                    ⋅
                    <!-- ⋅ -->
                   </mo>

               <math alttext="{\displaystyle {\begin{aligned}F_{x}&amp;=-q\left({\frac {\partial \phi }{\partial x}}+{\frac {\partial A_{x}}{\partial t}}\right)+q\left[{\dot {y}}\left({\frac {\partial A_{y}}{\partial x}}-{\frac {\partial A_{x}}{\partial y}}\right)+{\dot {z}}\left({\frac {\partial A_{z}}{\partial x}}-{\frac {\partial A_{x}}{\partial z}}\right)\right]\\&amp;=qE_{x}+q[{\dot {y}}(\nabla \times \mathbf {A} )_{z}-{\dot {z}}(\nabla \times \mathbf {A} )_{y}]\\&amp;=qE_{x}+q[\mathbf {\dot {r}} \times (\nabla \times \mathbf {A} )]_{x}\\&amp;=qE_{x}+q(\mathbf {\dot {r}} \times \mathbf {B} )_{x}\end{aligned}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

            and similarly for the

            directions. Hence the force equation is:
           </p>
